055338466X|2.0|Pr&eacute;cis: A brief introduction . . .<br /><br />In reading the other reviews of The Grand Design (\\"TGD\\"), including the top reviews, one conversant with these matters actually feels a moral incumbency to respond in reading their respective qualifications in dealing with the assertion that \\"philosophy is dead.\\"  The reviewers' qualification is that \\"philosophy is dead in the sense of answering the most mysterious of life's questions. It is up to science, and scientific theory, to provide clues to the true answers, as philosophy in its most ancient forms has taken a back seat, but modern philosophy, that of scientific philosophy, has taken root.\\"  Or, take the review that states \\"I was concerned by some of the things that were said at the outset such as \\"'philosophy is dead'\\" - each academic discipline requires years of study and can't reasonably be dismissed out of hand by someone who is an expert in another field - but my concerns were eased by the rest of the book.<br /><br />In other words, the \\"science\\" vindicates Hawking's sweeping statement(s).  As this lengthy review intends to prove, such conclusions are wrong, they entirely miss the point, and the content of TGD was entirely predictable given Hawking's previous materials.  TGD actually evidences that Hawking and Mlodinow have past the boundaries of extreme philosophical antirealism to the point of providing young-earth creationism justification on par with the lambda cold dark matter concordance model of the universe!--that should wake the reviewers up.<br /><br />For reason(s) argued below, this book lends itself to natural philosophy wrapped up with scientific speculation that reaches the boundaries of anti-realism (minus the usual nod to STR, GTR, quantum mechanisms and the Standard Model found in practically every other pop-science book).  These issues have been rehearsed ad infinitum.  After reading and re-reading the relevant aspects of The Grand Design, I would argue that it should contain the ISBN earmark placing it in the genre of philosophy/science section.<br /><br />In reading Stephen Hawking's books and other materials, it is easy to trace the progression of Hawking's thoughts and arguments as they relate the universe that are ultimately an expression of his worldview, scientific naturalism, and that lead to his public pronouncements that his is now an atheist, allegedly backed up by science as expressed in TGD--an issue that has nothing to do with science as this review/critique argues below.<br /><br />That is, Hawking's atheism has nothing to do with scientific discovery, theories, models or ad hoc assertions, but is premised upon very speculative and even rich philosophical views many of which are encapsulated by his TGD. Moreover, the alleged \\"shock value\\" of the Discovery channel's 2012 presentation of TGD (the Discovery program surprisingly focuses on this issue of \\"consciousness,\\" which is not a major topic in TGD but only a medium to their view; thankfully, as Hawking knows even less about philosophy of mind, a critique that is beyond the scope of this review). Hawking does logically reject free-will with some qualification(s).  That is, I agree that a lack of free-will is a broadly logical entailment based on his atheist/scientific naturalist worldview that Hawking endorses (similar to William Provine's reductionism) and applaud Hawking for the assertion.  Only the `open-minded' reader will unpack the ramifications of writing a book allegedly conveying the truth of reality when one lacks free-will, that only portends that the reader out to count TGD as imparting knowledge.  But I have gone too far.<br /><br />Like TGD, the Discovery Channel's program begins with this seemingly explosive statement that \\"philosophy is dead,\\" which is itself a philosophical statement also iterated in the pages of TGD.  It reminds one of the \\"Death of God\\" movement in the 1960's, derived from the death of its predecessors \\"logical positivism\\" and \\"verification of meaning.\\"<br /><br />Hawking's TGD is emblematic of one that has little to no philosophical (let alone theological) knowledge (although these disciplines are taken out of the category of knowledge through mere assumption.  However, many reviews--as evident from the top book reviews--do not present any critiques for a popular \\"science book,\\"  Instead one is left with a litany of \\"that-a-boy\\" pats on the back for what turns out to be a book that fails in its initial thesis, and enters into domains of deep philosophy that neither Hawking nor Mlodinow have any knowledge about contrary to their iterations and alleged arguments.  In short, the TGD not a book about science but an exercise in the philosophy of science and ontology, and an embarrassing one at that (e.g., The first one-third of TGD's is a philosophical discussion about scientific realism and antirealism), precisely because neither author are even acquainted with the basics in these fields, an argument this review unpacks below. No reason to take my word on this issue.<br /><br />According to NYU's Tim Maudlin, one of the most eminent and influential philosophers of spacetime and physics in the world, working at the number one American philosophy institution in the world, rightly begs to differ, not to mention Michael Redhead or D.H Mellor both of whom work at the same universities as the authors of TGD.  In an article in the `The Atlantic' Dr. Maudlin graciously states that, \\"Hawking is a brilliant man, but he's not an expert in what's going on in philosophy, evidently. Over the past thirty years the philosophy of physics has become seamlessly integrated with the foundations of physics work done by actual physicists, so the situation is actually the exact opposite of what he describes. I think he just doesn't know what he's talking about. I mean there's no reason why he should. Why should he spend a lot of time reading the philosophy of physics? I'm sure it's very difficult for him to do.  But I think he's just . . . uninformed.\\"  Maudlin is being too kind.  Sir Martin Rees, a friend of Hawking for 40 years confessed that Hawking, although a brilliant man, knows or reads little philosophy and even less theology, and TGD bears this out in spades.<br /><br />Hawking is not saying anything new regarding the issue of what statement(s) belong in the category of knowledge versus what the category of belief (and here I am bypassing more technical issues in epistemology to stay on point). For example, like Hawking's \\"philosophy is dead,\\" statement, including and science proves God does not exist abound in the public square. In Michio's Kaku's recent CNN interview, he iterates that the discovery of the Higgs actually \\"disproves religion\\" and \\"takes us back to the instant of creation itself.\\"  Both remarks that are patently false on their face and any philosophically informed physicist ought to cringe at such remarks.<br /><br />Since the publication of TGD, there have been a host of materials, essays and books that have grappled with TGD.  This review is influenced from my readings and reflections on TGD, and it also borrows from Walter Ray's \\"Is God Necessary?\\" (2012), Rondey Holder's \\"Big Bang, Big God,\\" John Lennox' debate with Hawking and his book \\"God and Stephen Hawking: Whose Design is it Anyway?\\" (2011), Bill Craig's 2010 article in `Philosophia Christi,' \\"Much Ado About Nothing: A Review Essay in The Grand Design\\" less formal aspects of which can be derived from his website, and a 2011 conference on this issue with both Rodney Holder and Bill Craig [minus Stephen Hawking who was invited to Saint Andrews] once can locate on reasonablefaith.org.<br /><br />I cannot be accused of only reading books by those contrary to Hawking's TGD as I typically read primary materials per the parting advice of my graduate teachers. This can be confirmed by reviewing my on-line profile and the books that I have purchased, which are behind the real-time materials available on either edge.com or ArXiv.org. Unfortunately, there are few informed scientific naturalists that would go out on a limb and defend Hawking's views in TGD (including the publisher that I first thought was backed by John Brockman).  These materials will be referred to throughout the remainder of the review and critique.  Any rebutting or undercutting defectors to the resources cited, and I have read none, have no applicability to the following critique.<br /><br />Part 1: Review/Critique . . .<br /><br />Stephen Hawking and Leonard Mlodinow open TGD with woefully arrogant quotes about the death of philosophy (and ultimately God) by announcing that \\". . . philosophy is dead\\" because \\"Philosophy has not kept up with modern developments in science, particularly physics. Scientists have become the bearers of the torch of discovery in our quest for knowledge.  The purpose of the torch of discovery in our quest for knowledge\\" (pg. 5).  Again, this is an expression of what counts as knowledge (\\"we scientists\\"), and everything else is outside the domain of our knowledge (defined here traditionally as justified true, belief) is mere simple belief.  But they go further as their project: given the death of disciplines such as philosophy, TGD is a program that tries to assume one of these disciplines under the umbrella of science--the corpse of philosophy.<br /><br />After pronouncing the death of philosophy, the authors begin TGD by iterating their intent to answer three of the most profound questions ever asked, which comprises the remainder of the book:<br /><br />1. Why is there something rather than nothing?<br />2. Why do we exist?<br />3. Why this particular set of laws and not some other?<br /><br />I believe that I mentioned the first one-third of the author's book is a discussion of realism versus antirealism.  After re-reading TGD, I now believe that almost 80% of TGD is philosophical in content and ought to be regarded as a book on natural philosophy versus science.  There is an irony here for atheist/scientific naturalists that are bothered by Christian theist philosopher that utilize the findings of science to strengthen certain premises in arguing for the existence of God. Hawking and Mlodinow are equally culpable in TGD (like many other unnamed atheist/scientific naturalists) of doing the same in answering these questions above.<br /><br />Let's jump right into this philosophical world painted with a `scientific brush' that many of the reviewers are seemingly unaware, or his \\"model-dependent realism\\" (\\"MIR\\").  Let me back up a bit and place this squarely in the domain they claim is dead.  Hawking and Mlodinow are actually espousing a view of philosophy called \\"ontological pluralism.\\"  I first learned of this term from philosopher Dr. William Craig and there is little literature on this view as this is a minority and extreme view.<br />Ontological pluralism states that there is \\"no right answer too many if not most ontological questions,\\" or questions about reality and the nature of reality.  Most ontological pluralists reserve their philosophy what are high level disputes.  For example, let's say someone asks `Does the Sun exist?'  According to the view, there is no right answer but merely different ways of describing reality, none of which is more correct or more accurate than the other (page 7).<br /><br />To use Craig's example, \\"So if you were to ask, `Is there such a thing as the moon?,' the ontological pluralist would say that the question has no objective answer.\\"  Again, if an ontological pluralist is asked, \\"Is there such thing as a moon?\\", they say this question has no objective meaning and that it is both true the moon exists and it is not true that the moon exists.<br />How does this relate to MIR?  Unlike ontological pluralism, MIR is actually more extreme in that Hawking and Mlodinow extend their view to one's `apprehension of the world' and go beyond the ontological pluralist by not only agreeing with their view but alsoadding that our entire apprehension of the world is also part of model-dependent realism.<br /><br />They is to say, (and here I freely borrow from Bill Craig's `Much Ado About Nothing' stated above), \\". . . our brains interpret the input from our sensory organs by making a model of the world. When such a model is successful at explaining events, we tend to attribute to it, and to the elements and concepts that constitute it, the quality of reality or absolute truth. But there may be different ways in which one could model the same physical situation, with each employing different fundamental elements and concepts. If two such physical theories or models accurately predict the same events, one cannot be said to be more real than the other; rather, we are free to use which ever model is most convenient\\" (pg. 7)<br />.<br />That is, their skeptical model, in part, is an unconscious way of organizing sense perceptions, which can be refined by scientific theorizing (another assumption without argument). We never come to know the way the world is; all we achieve are more or less convenient ways of organizing our perceptions.  Such skepticism would be bad enough; but the situation is even worse. For these various models are not, even unbeknownst to us, more or less accurate approximations of reality. Rather there is no objective reality to which our models more or less accurately correspond.  How does this relate to MIR?  In short, MIR is full-blown ontological pluralism: there is no objective reality to which our models accurately correspond.  Therefore, it follows that Hawking and Mlodinow are extreme antirealists and TGD is not a pop-science book, but a book on issues that neither author has any clue on which they are writing.<br /><br />What about that scientific brush, such as the one the Discovery Channel creatively tried to paint?  According to Craig and Rodney Holder (see my book review of `Big Bang, Big God'), Hawking and Mlodinow try to distinguish their view from scientific anti-realism by defining the latter as the view that \\"observation and experiment are meaningful but that theories are no more than useful instruments that do not embody any deeper truths underlying the observed phenomena\\" (pg. 44).<br /><br />What Hawking and Mlodinow are describing here, however, is actually positivism, a debunked philosophy of science popular in the 1930s and 1940s. Positivism proved to be untenable in part because of its artificial distinction between observation statements and theoretical statements.  Hawking's anti-realism does not depend on positivism as Hawking and Mlodinow are actually more anti-realist than the positivists, for they not only deny that theoretical statements express objective truths about the world, but they also deny the same of observation statements as well, since even observation is model-dependent.  Again, what they're denying is not just knowledge of the way the world is, but the fact that there even is an objective world to be known.<br />To unpack this with an example from their book, just in case the reviewer finds themselves in disbelief as to the extent of Hawking and Mlodinow's antirealism, one of the most striking examples is derived from their \\"thoughtful goldfish\\"  If a goldfish viewing the world through a curved bowl could formulate a model that enabled it to make successful predictions, then \\"we would have to admit the goldfish's view as a valid picture of reality\\" (pg. 39). Moreover, Ptolemy's geocentric model of the world was just as adequate as Copernicus system?  Hawking and Mlodinow say that Copernicus proved Ptolemy wrong, this is \\"not true\\" (pg. 41).  This has nothing to do with the evidence.  Rather, given MIR, the authors are stating that neither model is objectively true.  As stated in the first paragraph, the authors go even further in contrasting young earth creationism and the big bang theory and claim that, although the big bang model is \\"more useful,\\" neither model can be said tobe more real than the other\\" (pg. 51).  The fact that our observations are model-dependent and/or theory-laden does not imply that we can't have knowledge of the world, including there is no knowledge of the way the world is.  Is this science?<br /><br />Hawking's arguments sound as if we are getting close to Max Tegmark's peculiar ontology as set forth in his \\"Our Mathematical Universe: My Quest for the Ultimate Nature of Reality.\\"  I have not yet read the contents, but the reviews say enough to make the analogy as Hawking and Mlodinow offer up the that fact that, if we were possible inhabitants of a virtual reality controlled by alien beings, then there would be no way for us to tell that we were in the simulated world and so we would have no reason to doubt its reality (pg. 42).  Here I am reminded and concurrently emboldened by the extremes that physicists/cosmologists go in justifying their naturalism-of-the-gaps.<br /><br />This reminds me Paul Davies' view that in entertaining the possibility of a multiverse, there is no good reason to rule out universes that contain \\"computer simulations\\" of other universes.  What Davies means is that \\"in a multiverse [Tegmark's level II through IV universes] technological civilizations like ours will emerge in some subset of universes, and civilizations more technologically advanced than ours will attain the capacity to simulate consciousness.\\"  Davies continues that it is only a small leap to simulations of conscious beings to simulate an entire virtual world for them to inhabit (pg. 152, quoting pg. 496 of Paul Davies article `Universes Galore: Where Will it all End' from Universe or Multiverse? ed. by Bernard Carr).<br /><br />Even closer, and a view justified by MIR. philosopher and self-proclaimed 'transhumanist' Nick Bostrom's who takes Davies' view a step further concluding that the reader is very likely a computer simulation (Bostrom's `simulation argument')  and we could be the simulations of the human ancestors of post-humans, rather than real humans. Using an argument from probability Bostrom concludes that simulations are likely to be overwhelmingly dominant and that we should conclude that our universe is more likely to be a simulation than a real universe; and, \\"we are therefore not real biological persons but simulated ones\\" (pg. 153).  These are extant options in present cosmology and ones the authors of TGD should readily embrace (see Amanda's Geftar's new book \\"Trespassing on Einstein's Lawn\\" [a well-written book] pgs. 77-84 for more on Bostrom and the defeaters and the authors fascination with this \\"brain-in-a-vat\\" viewpoint).<br /><br />Regarding the virtual reality controlled by aliens, other authors rightly argue that this argument does not exclude the possibility that we have in this case two competing theories of reality--the aliens and our own--and logically speaking one view is true and one is false even if we are unable to tell which is which.  This is science?  Perhaps those reviewers ought to re-read TGD.<br />Hawking and Mlodinow go further in their epistemic/ontological confusion(s), especially when they \\"assert\\" without argument that \\"There is no model-independent test of reality.  It follows that a well-constructed model creates a reality of its own\\" (pg. 172).  Here we have an assertion of a more well-known philosophical view that reality is different for persons having different models, or relativism (not to be confused with relativity).<br /><br />Therefore, for Andre Linde an eternal inflationary model is real.  For Roger Penrose twister theory is the true model.  For Martin Bojowald's, loop quantum gravity model is real.  According to Hawking and Mlodinow, there is no iff and they are all equally \\"real!\\"  How?  Because they claim that the model itself is responsible for creating its respective reality.  But how does this follow when there is no model-independent test of the way the world is.  This reminds me of postmodernist, Stanley Fish, holding up his book \\"Is There a Text on This Class?\\" trying to prove that there is no such thing as authorial intent; hence, no text--a tad self-referentially self-incoherent as the purpose of Fish's book is to make this very point.<br /><br />The analogy is one from negation, but the main point should be obvious to the reviewer--despite the so-called death of philosophy, TGD is a book steeped in philosophy and the authors are positing views from philosophy and the science of philosophy that are regarded as extreme antirealism.  There is no rebutting or undercutting defeater(s) to this argument.  One wonders why there are no endorsements on the back of TGD's cover related to TGD.  I would gather that, despite the selling point (i.e., Hawking's name), the editor likely realized that the most important conclusions are philosophical and not scientific, which is what the remainder of this paper intends to bear out.<br /><br />For the reviewer that may have grown weary long ago, I predict the likely replies will be personal attacks, or to pick out some issue I have raised out of context and try to defeat the argument, despite the fact that Part 1 is an entire argument.  Good luck as this is representative of your worldview, and I am not bashing it with posts to other books or presupposing Christianity in my review.<br /><br />Part 2: The Laws of Nature . . .<br /><br />This section of the review intends to discuss the author's view of a law of nature (pgs. 27-34) and critique the responses three questions they raise (1); what is the origin of these laws? (2) Are there any exceptions to the laws such as miracles; and, (3) is there only one set of possible laws  The next edit/update will follow the end of this argument.  (This critique is not finished and Part 2 will be unpacked within a week).<br /><br />Regarding (1) the authors aver that God established nature's laws, but then they take issue with God's attributes, despite the fact that God's attributes are both well-defined and argued in books by more well-informed atheists.  For TGD's authors, they complain that the \\"God\\" answer is qualitatively equivalent to defining God as the \\"embodiment of the laws of [nature).\\"  The authors are historically mistaken on this point which only lends credence to the argument that they lack basic philosophical knowledge.  They state that it was Descartes--views that actually belong to Spinoza--that posited that nature's laws are freely willed by God.  In fact, Spinoza regarded god and nature as the same, which is in contradistinction of even the most basic books of philosophical theology and the attributes of God as having attributes, both communicable and incommunicable, that distinguish God from nature.  This evidences both their lack of philosophical knowledge and theology, and should be an embarrassment to anyone that endorses TGD as you likely fall into the same category.<br /><br />Regarding issue (2) the authors build their house on sand regarding the issue of \\"miracles,\\" a cuss word for the naturalist devoid of understanding first class and second class miracles, as the \\"real crunch\\" (and we are not referring of Aristotelian causes).  The writers provide a perfect example of such fallacious thinking.  First, in responding whether there are miracles or lack thereof [which are never defined], they look back to the Christian's response and definition of (1) which is borderline incoherent and one they answer in the negative.  So what's the rub?  Pretend I am a deist (like Anthony Flew before he passed [please bypass the statements of senility and that his book was ghost written, which are false and irrelevant]) believing that God established a Newtonian clockwork universe and choosing not to intervene.  By this mere locution I have already dismantled their argument as, broadly logically speaking, there can be no \\"real crunch\\" in providing an answer to (1) by God, and (2) by iterating \\"No!\\"  If the reader does not understand this minor point, thenTGD is the perfect philosophy book for you.  Pardon the incivility, but the village atheists and the \\"professional reviewers\\" can move even an educated Christian into \\"what are you thinking mode\\"?\\"<br /><br />So why do Hawking and Mlodinow answer (2) negatively?  There answer, like many reviews, that presuppose metaphysical naturalism, is that \\"science requires it.\\"  They wrongly state that the \\"scientific naturalism that Laplace formulated is the modern scientists' answer to questions (2). [That is] in fact, the basis of all modern science, and a principle that is important throughout this book.  A scientific law is not a scientific law if it holds only when some [ultra mundane] being decides not to intervene\\" (pg. 30). (I will ignore the metaphysical and methodological issue until my nest post).<br /><br />In response, (i) I would assume the authors' of TGD's intellectual caliber would know their own history, but TGD does not bear this out as Laplacian determinism is not the basis of modern science, even as the historic/ontic footnote the authors iterate.  That is, Hawking and Mlodinow regard the indeterminacy characteristic of quantum physics as ontic.  And if nature is indetermistic the determinism of Laplacian's Newtonian determinacy fails. Laplacean determinism contradicts quantum indeterminacy, which brings one to the second objection; (ii) the authors of TGD regard the indeterminacy of as ontological (not epistemic) characteristic of quantum mechanics (an issue beyond the scope of this review), then Laplace's determinism contradicts their initial assertion as there is a contradiction between their affirmation of Laplacian determinism and quantum indeterminacy.<br /><br />The (iii) criticism is borrowed from Bill Craig; namely, the authors confuse \\"determinism\\" with \\"naturalism.\\" Hawking and Mlodinow present an argument against first-class miracles via a personal ultra mundane being.  As Craig states, [t]heir argument against the intervention of a supernatural being is an argument for naturalism, not determinism.  Quantum indeterminacy is acceptable because it is naturalistic, where as miracles involve supernatural agency.\\"  Craig continues that that their claim is false: that scientific laws would not be \\"laws\\" if they hold only when a supernatural being decides not to intervene, which is false as the laws of nature describe the behavior of physical systems in the absence of any supernatural intervention.  \\"Were a supernatural agent to intervene, the predictions based on the laws would not hold precisely because non-natural factors, not envisioned by the laws would not hold precisely because non-natural factors, not envisioned by the laws, have entered this picture.  The laws thus have implicit ceteris paribus [\\"all things being equal\\"] conditions: they describe the behavior of physical systems given that no supernatural agent intervenes.  If such a being does intervene, the natural law is not abrogated, since it describes the behavior of the system only under the assumption that such a being does not intervene\\" (`Much Ado About Nothing,' pg. 413) [emphasis added].<br /><br />And (iv), it would have been more respectable if the authors would have, at a minimum, mentioned that there are numerous alternatives to determinism (a minority view).  The unfamiliar reader would have no idea that these issues are multitudinous; hence, there is no free will, is nowhere close to the end of the discussion contrary to the plethora of new books that make the try and make the same argument, but all presuppose materialism.<br /><br />Part 3: Free Will . . .<br /><br />If this sounds confused, and it is, let's leave aside these issues for the moment and reflect on their views related to free-will.  In short, TGD argues that as people live in the universe and interact within the universe that scientific determinism must hold for people (pg. 30), humans are nothing more than biological machines and free will is an illusion (pg. 32).  (i)This is what Plantinga calls a \\"philosophical tar baby.\\"  That is to say, if we lack free will then why or how do the authors think that anything they have iterated in their book is actually true, especially given that everything is merely a product of blind physical causes?  Their argument against free-will is based on neo-Darwinism.  According to the authors \\"If we have free will, where in the evolutionary tree did [free will] develop?\\"  This would be a great place to unpack Alvin Plantinga's \\"evolutionary argument against naturalism\\" but it is beyond our review (although I can envision the so-called enlightened thinker at pains to try and rebut this latter assertion).  If so, then good--welcome to the culture war.<br /><br />The authors do present an argument/assertion, which Hawking has written in previous materials: free will is simply an illusion because neurosurgeons can stimulate a person's brain in a manner to produce a desire for one to move their limbs. (ii) This statement about creating a \\"desire\\" ought to have theistic, atheist, and agnostic philosophers scratching their head regarding these broadly logical entailments as Hawking has included immaterial mental states.  That is, to say, stimulating the brain can cause one to raise their arm is a standard deterministic view, but to argue that stimulation creates desires is a wholly different assertion altogether.<br /><br />(iii) Whether having free will has nothing to dependence on specification where one obtains free will in the evolutionary process, and such a remark is close to a categorical fallacy.  The standard neo-Darwinian \\"just-so\\" story regarding consciousness is that free will arose when the brain evolved in complexity creating consciousness, qualia, intentionality, etc.  For the initiated, such a sweeping statement is a mare's nest I invite the initiated to challenge, although it has nothing to do with TGD's arguments.<br /><br />As this paper is being published in another context, I will provide the prompt to obtain the article in about 1 monthbe more real than the other\\" (pg. 51).  The fact that our observations are model-dependent and/or theory-laden does not imply that we can't have knowledge of the world, including there is no knowledge of the way the world is.  Is this science?<br /><br />Hawking's arguments sound as if we are getting close to Max Tegmark's peculiar ontology as set forth in his \\"Our Mathematical Universe: My Quest for the Ultimate Nature of Reality.\\"  I have not yet read the contents, but the reviews say enough to make the analogy as Hawking and Mlodinow offer up the that fact that, if we were possible inhabitants of a virtual reality controlled by alien beings, then there would be no way for us to tell that we were in the simulated world and so we would have no reason to doubt its reality (pg. 42).  Here I am reminded and concurrently emboldened by the extremes that physicists/cosmologists go in justifying their naturalism-of-the-gaps.<br /><br />This reminds me Paul Davies' view that in entertaining the possibility of a multiverse, there is no good reason to rule out universes that contain \\"computer simulations\\" of other universes.  What Davies means is that \\"in a multiverse [Tegmark's level II through IV universes] technological civilizations like ours will emerge in some subset of universes, and civilizations more technologically advanced than ours will attain the capacity to simulate consciousness.\\"  Davies continues that it is only a small leap to simulations of conscious beings to simulate an entire virtual world for them to inhabit (pg. 152, quoting pg. 496 of Paul Davies article `Universes Galore: Where Will it all End' from Universe or Multiverse? ed. by Bernard Carr).<br /><br />Even closer, and a view justified by MIR. philosopher and self-proclaimed 'transhumanist' Nick Bostrom's who takes Davies' view a step further concluding that the reader is very likely a computer simulation (Bostrom's `simulation argument')  and we could be the simulations of the human ancestors of post-humans, rather than real humans. Using an argument from probability Bostrom concludes that simulations are likely to be overwhelmingly dominant and that we should conclude that our universe is more likely to be a simulation than a real universe; and, \\"we are therefore not real biological persons but simulated ones\\" (pg. 153).  These are extant options in present cosmology and ones the authors of TGD should readily embrace (see Amanda's Geftar's new book \\"Trespassing on Einstein's Lawn\\" [a well-written book] pgs. 77-84 for more on Bostrom and the defeaters and the authors fascination with this \\"brain-in-a-vat\\" viewpoint).<br /><br />Regarding the virtual reality controlled by aliens, other authors rightly argue that this argument does not exclude the possibility that we have in this case two competing theories of reality--the aliens and our own--and logically speaking one view is true and one is false even if we are unable to tell which is which.  This is science?  Perhaps those reviewers ought to re-read TGD.<br />Hawking and Mlodinow go further in their epistemic/ontological confusion(s), especially when they \\"assert\\" without argument that \\"There is no model-independent test of reality.  It follows that a well-constructed model creates a reality of its own\\" (pg. 172).  Here we have an assertion of a more well-known philosophical view that reality is different for persons having different models, or relativism (not to be confused with relativity).<br /><br />Therefore, for Andre Linde an eternal inflationary model is real.  For Roger Penrose twister theory is the true model.  For Martin Bojowald's, loop quantum gravity model is real.  According to Hawking and Mlodinow, there is no iff and they are all equally \\"real!\\"  How?  Because they claim that the model itself is responsible for creating its respective reality.  But how does this follow when there is no model-independent test of the way the world is.  This reminds me of postmodernist, Stanley Fish, holding up his book \\"Is There a Text on This Class?\\" trying to prove that there is no such thing as authorial intent; hence, no text--a tad self-referentially self-incoherent as the purpose of Fish's book is to make this very point.<br /><br />The analogy is one from negation, but the main point should be obvious to the reviewer--despite the so-called death of philosophy, TGD is a book steeped in philosophy and the authors are positing views from philosophy and the science of philosophy that are regarded as extreme antirealism.  There is no rebutting or undercutting defeater(s) to this argument.  One wonders why there are no endorsements on the back of TGD's cover related to TGD.  I would gather that, despite the selling point (i.e., Hawking's name), the editor likely realized that the most important conclusions are philosophical and not scientific, which is what the remainder of this paper intends to bear out.<br /><br />For the reviewer that may have grown weary long ago, I predict the likely replies will be personal attacks, or to pick out some issue I have raised out of context and try to defeat the argument, despite the fact that Part 1 is an entire argument.  Good luck as this is representative of your worldview, and I am not bashing it with posts to other books or presupposing Christianity in my review.<br /><br />Part 2: The Laws of Nature . . .<br /><br />This section of the review intends to discuss the author's view of a law of nature (pgs. 27-34) and critique the responses three questions they raise (1); what is the origin of these laws? (2) Are there any exceptions to the laws such as miracles; and, (3) is there only one set of possible laws  The next edit/update will follow the end of this argument.  (This critique is not finished and Part 2 will be unpacked within a week).<br /><br />Regarding (1) the authors aver that God established nature's laws, but then they take issue with God's attributes, despite the fact that God's attributes are both well-definedand argued in books by more well-informed atheists.  For TGD's authors, they complain that the \\"God\\" answer is qualitatively equivalent to defining God as the \\"embodiment of the laws of [nature).\\"  The authors are historically mistaken on this point which only lends credence to the argument that they lack basic philosophical knowledge.  They state that it was Descartes--views that actually belong to Spinoza--that posited that nature's laws are freely willed by God.  In fact, Spinoza regarded god and nature as the same, which is in contradistinction of even the most basic books of philosophical theology and the attributes of God as having attributes, both communicable and incommunicable, that distinguish God from nature.  This evidences both their lack of philosophical knowledge and theology, and should be an embarrassment to anyone that endorses TGD as you likely fall into the same category.<br /><br />Regarding issue (2) the authors build their house on sand regarding the issue of \\"miracles,\\" a cuss word for the naturalist devoid of understanding first class and second class miracles, as the \\"real crunch\\" (and we are not referring of Aristotelian causes).  The writers provide a perfect example of such fallacious thinking.  First, in responding whether there are miracles or lack thereof [which are never defined], they look back to the Christian's response and definition of (1) which is borderline incoherent and one they answer in the negative.  So what's the rub?  Pretend I am a deist (like Anthony Flew before he passed [please bypass the statements of senility and that his book was ghost written, which are false and irrelevant]) believing that God established a Newtonian clockwork universe and choosing not to intervene.  By this mere locution I have already dismantled their argument as, broadly logically speaking, there can be no \\"real crunch\\" in providing an answer to (1) by God, and (2) by iterating \\"No!\\"  If the reader does not understand this minor point, thenTGD is the perfect philosophy book for you.  Pardon the incivility, but the village atheists and the \\"professional reviewers\\" can move even an educated Christian into \\"what are you thinking mode\\"?\\"<br /><br />So why do Hawking and Mlodinow answer (2) negatively?  There answer, like many reviews, that presuppose metaphysical naturalism, is that \\"science requires it.\\"  They wrongly state that the \\"scientific naturalism that Laplace formulated is the modern scientists' answer to questions (2). [That is] in fact, the basis of all modern science, and a principle that is important throughout this book.  A scientific law is not a scientific law if it holds only when some [ultra mundane] being decides not to intervene\\" (pg. 30). (I will ignore the metaphysical and methodological issue until my nest post).<br /><br />In response, (i) I would assume the authors' of TGD's intellectual caliber would know their own history, but TGD does not bear this out as Laplacian determinism is not the basis of modern science, even as the historic/ontic footnote the authors iterate.  That is, Hawking and Mlodinow regard the indeterminacy characteristic of quantum physics as ontic.  And if nature is indetermistic the determinism of Laplacian's Newtonian determinacy fails. Laplacean determinism contradicts quantum indeterminacy, which brings one to the second objection; (ii) the authors of TGD regard the indeterminacy of as ontological (not epistemic) characteristic of quantum mechanics (an issue beyond the scope of this review), then Laplace's determinism contradicts their initial assertion as there is a contradiction between their affirmation of Laplacian determinism and quantum indeterminacy.<br /><br />The (iii) criticism is borrowed from Bill Craig; namely, the authors confuse \\"determinism\\" with \\"naturalism.\\" Hawking and Mlodinow present an argument against first-class miracles via a personal ultra mundane being.  As Craig states, [t]heir argument against the intervention of a supernatural being is an argument for naturalism, not determinism.  Quantum indeterminacy is acceptable because it is naturalistic, where as miracles involve supernatural agency.\\"  Craig continues that that their claim is false: that scientific laws would not be \\"laws\\" if they hold only when a supernatural being decides not to intervene, which is false as the laws of nature describe the behavior of physical systems in the absence of any supernatural intervention.  \\"Were a supernatural agent to intervene, the predictions based on the laws would not hold precisely because non-natural factors, not envisioned by the laws would not hold precisely because non-natural factors, not envisioned by the laws, have entered this picture.  The laws thus have implicit ceteris paribus [\\"all things being equal\\"] conditions: they describe the behavior of physical systems given that no supernatural agent intervenes.  If such a being does intervene, the natural law is not abrogated, since it describes the behavior of the system only under the assumption that such a being does not intervene\\" (`Much Ado About Nothing,' pg. 413) [emphasis added].<br /><br />And (iv), it would have been more respectable if the authors would have, at a minimum, mentioned that there are numerous alternatives to determinism (a minority view).  The unfamiliar reader would have no idea that these issues are multitudinous; hence, there is no free will, is nowhere close to the end of the discussion contrary to the plethora of new books that make the try and make the same argument, but all presuppose materialism.<br /><br />Part 3: Free Will . . .<br /><br />If this sounds confused, and it is, let's leave aside these issues for the moment and reflect on their views related to free-will. 0786956291|2.0|While this box set tries to entice new players into the world of Dungeons & Dragons, you the buyer, has to beware that this is NOT the game itself.  This box set contains enough rules and items to take you through a couple of the enclosed encounters, in sort of a 'pick a path' type adventure setting.  If you are a player from back in the day, trying to get the Basic (Moldvay/Mentzer) set and have some fun again...Steer clear of this.  If you are a new player who wants to play the game that Vin Diesel, Seth Green, Mathew Lilliard and Michelle Rodriquez play...Steer clear of this.    The problem with this box set is that it will bog the new player down about halfway through the character creation process.  With all the additional rules needed for ability checks and other game mechanics, it feels more like a tactical miniatures game with tons of charts and rules to cross check and reference.  New players unfamiliar with the game will lose interest in all this geek-ness of checking charts, rolling for every action under the sun and having to figure out tactical maneuvers on top of it.  My guess, real new players will drop this fast.    You would be better served to look around on Amazon/eBay for the original D&D rule sets from the 1980's and enjoy a looser, fast paced adventure game that not only explains the game better, but wont bog you down with a mortgage on your house trying to collect and buy all the 4e books and supplements.    This box set is for people that played 3.0-3.5 version of D&D and want to even further busy up their gaming session by diving into the minutiae that is 4e.  It is NOT for totally new players.    Finally, if your reading this far, thank you for indulging me.  This set has a nice box, complete with cover art from the 1980's 'Mentzer' set (highly recommended) and as for quality, that is about it.  The box IS NICE!  The interior game pieces...Not so nice.  The rule books are printed on something that feels so flimsy that I doubt it will last past one session.  Water or any other stains will ruin this, I am betting even the printing will run.  Everything else looks like it was done on the cheap, but the dice are not so bad for a beginners set.  You will want to Google Game Science dice of course for real, big boy grown up dice.    Due to the cheap interior contents and the confusing myriad of rules, I cannot recommend this set to the totally novice player.  If you are a 3.0/3.5 player trying to get into 4e, this might be of interest...After all, its a cheap way for you to realize your better off sticking with 3.0/3.5.

0618918248|4.0|A lot of people get fired up about this book, so I wanted to read it to try to see what the fuss is about. And from many of the reactions I've seen to Dawkins, I had the impression that he must be veritably livid himself - all but foaming at the mouth as he spits cruel, cruel attacks at his poor (religious) victims.<br /><br />That really isn't what I found in The God Delusion.<br /><br />Of course, Dawkins doesn't write with the cool detachment and (extreme) caution of a good philosopher; he's a scientist, and he writes with a scientist's frustration in the face of a too-common dearth of reasoned thought and scientific literacy in lay society. Yes, the frustration shows through, but I don't see why anyone should fault him for that. People have committed gross atrocities in the name of \\"God\\" -- that is rightly very frustrating. But the book is not written in the style of a polemic; it's a reasoned argument, and it has the feel of such at almost every point. The humor is a nice break now and then, and he does get a bit quote-happy sometimes - but the quotes are interesting, so I enjoyed them as well.<br /><br />One thing I noticed was that I had to keep reminding myself that Dawkins was using the word \\"God\\" in a specific way; early in the book he explains precisely what he means by \\"the God hypothesis\\" and thereby what he means by \\"God\\". In a nutshell, that is a supernatural intelligent being that designed and created the universe and everything in it. This is a basic (many religious people would probably want to add to it) but common definition (this is kind of an essential - gets the essence of it - concept of God that many, even across religions, would agree is true of their God).<br /><br />(Side-note: there is a philosophical/logical problem with the notion of a supernatural entity fiddling around with the physical universe, and that's why I had to keep reminding myself that Dawkins was arguing against God as commonly conceived - otherwise I wouldn't see why he's cold as he is toward agnosticism.)<br /><br />Of course, if you think \\"God\\" is some kind of pattern in nature (or is nature itself), then Dawkins' arguments aren't going to work against your \\"God\\" - but you're also not talking about the God described in the scriptures of the major world religions, you're not talking about a personal God who created the universe and listens to your prayers and gave commandments and rules etc. to prophets ... in short, you're not talking about the kind of God that most people talk about, or go to church to worship, or believe works miracles from time to time, or in whose name people have committed atrocities. That is the kind of God that Dawkins is arguing against.<br /><br />And he does a fine job of it. Not a perfect job, but then I doubt I'd say that anyone has done a perfect job of arguing their point on any difficult and debated position. In the chapter on morality, it felt clear that he is not a moral philosopher - but that's probably a good thing, as moral philosophers can't even manage to agree on whether moral statements (like \\"it is wrong to kill\\") mean anything at all. Dawkins OTOH is writing for people in the real world. ;)<br /><br />His chapter arguing that religion is akin to child abuse sounded like it would be too extreme, but on reading it, a lot of what he had to say made a lot of sense. I grew up in an area with a lot of Amish, and Dawkins does strike pretty hard at them - but it seemed fair, and his condemnation of the rest of us for helping to forcefully perpetuate the culture seemed more so. I know the feeling of lament that we often have about old traditions dying out (particularly when they aren't our own traditions); but I also have to wonder why we should lament the fading of outdated traditions more than we lament the limited life possibilities available to the actual people who are trapped unwittingly or even grudgingly in those traditions. Dawkins rightly calls us out on this.<br /><br />...I still don't understand why people get so angry about The God Delusion, though. It's an argument, and the great thing about arguments is that if you disagree, you can try to dissect the argument and prove it wrong (or show why your own argument is stronger or more cogent). You can learn a lot from an argument whether you think it's right or wrong - so why get mad?ugh. You can learn a lot from an argument whether you think it's right or wrong - so why get mad?

0316098337|4.0|Emotionally drained and grieving.  There had to be space between myself and The Room before I could write a review.  This story is almost overwhelming; so visceral and emotionally raw that it hurts.  It bores into your soul, and resonnates with that 5 yr. old child we once were, and/or the parent that we are now, igniting that primordial fight or flight instinct born from absolute Fear.  My pragmatism has returned, so now I write. But there remains within me the frighteningly dark and ugly knowledge...that monsters are real.    A completely original premise, brave and authentic, but the power and brilliance of this novel lies not in what is written, but rather in what is BETWEEN the LINES! With the convincing innocense and naivete of a child, Jack narrates this story--without telling us the story at all!  Ex: Hidden in the wardrobe, impatient to be with Ma, Jack passes time counting the squeaks of the bed, his teeth = Ma is being raped by her captor; \\"Ma is switched off today,\\" = Ma is so depressed and desperate that she lies on the bed, almost catatonic... Donoghue has brilliantly enlisted the reader to bring this monster to life; it is our personal knowledge, and imaginings of evil and helplessness, that tells the real story.      I've read some criticism about the supporting characters being sketchy and undimensional.  But this tight focus on Ma and Jack separated them from the others, and created the claustrophbic imprisonment from the real world. There are some inconsistencies with Jack. One page has him spouting 6 syllable words and discussing scientific principles, then he digresses to a rudimentary explaination \\"something comes out of her mouth,\\" when Ma vomits. But, Jack is from a make-believe world, where consistency is an illusory anchor to sanity, provided by Ma. Does the same Ma that spends days in bed unable to function set out to systematically create a little genius?  Does she cognitively educate Jack, or does she just say aloud everything she knows,a frantic challenge to herself, like prisoners of war remember and repeat times tables.     If this was the Book Olympics I would be holding up the 10 sign. A fabulous book, but it is hard to read and is not for everyone.  I'm glad I read it, but almost feel weighted down by the the haunting story.
0393970116|4.0|\\"Howard's End\\" is a typical \\"English\\" novel that takes the tried and true form established by such authors as the Brontes and Austen, and subverts it in subtle and intrinsically 20th Century ways. There is romance, heartache, a house in the country, a clash of the classes, and large quirky families with characters both loveable and deplorable.  Yet while Forster includes all the standard elements in \\"Howard's End\\" (named after the aforementioned house in the country- a typical English author move), the themes and tone of this story takes the tradition in a new and thought-provoking direction.<br />  The title refers to a house in the country owned by the wealthy and, in many ways, out of touch Wilcoxes.  Early in the novel, the Wilcoxes cross paths with a pair of intellectual, forward thinking sisters, and despite having nothing in common with each other, the sisters and Wilcoxes begin a series of encounters that lead to the eventual collision of their personal lives.  On the fringes of the action, yet existing at the very core of the novel and its themes, lurk the Basts, a lower working class couple who's very lives and livelihoods are essentially dependent on the actions of both the Wilcoxes (the rich aristocracy) and the sisters (the compassionate liberals looking out for the poor).  These three sets of typically English characters collide and interact in a series of coincidences that never seem forced (thanks to the brilliance of Forster), and move the plot in what, when all is said and done, turns out to be an inevitability.<br />  Completed in 1910, \\"Howard's End\\" is very much a book of the 20th century. Themes of socialism and class struggle, while never explicit, underlie the actions and dilemmas of the characters.  Forster's writing is brilliant, and the conversations between the \\"bleeding heart liberal\\" sisters and the cold hearted business oriented Wilcoxes capture the \\"english novel\\" vibe so dead-on that at times Forster can almost be accussed of mocking them both.  In a lesser author's hands, this novel could have been nothing more than farce, but Forster walks that fine line between satire and homage and produces a masterpiece that in many ways signals the end of the \\"English aristocracy\\" and its stranglehold on English fiction.<br />  This is a complex, worthwhile read!em both.  In a lesser author's hands, this novel could have been nothing more than farce, but Forster walks that fine line between satire and homage and produces a masterpiece that in many ways signals the end of the \\"English aristocracy\\" and its stranglehold on English fiction.     This is a complex, worthwhile read!

0553805371|4.0|Traditionally, science never claimed to deal with \\"why\\" questions. Philosophers, and many scientists, seemed content with relegating scientific concerns exclusively to the realm of \\"how.\\" Science, so this claim went, could never address life's meaning. Answers to perennial questions such as \\"why are we here?\\" or \\"why does anything exist at all?\\" would never appear in microscope lenses or in the atom splitter's detritus. Times have of course changed. \\"Pure thought\\" and \\"pure logic\\" have given way to mathematical and theoretical inference and observational confirmation. \\"The Grand Design,\\" a book that, 400 years ago, authorities would have thrown into the flames - and maybe its authors as well - attempts to answer the \\"big questions\\" in the framework of scientific theory. Bold claims follow. Right away the book proclaims, without hesitation, that \\"philosophy is dead.\\" Even a cursory examination of modern academic philosophy suggests this, but the statement nonetheless resonates with some arrogance. In cosmological terms, yes, current philosophy provides as much value as zero Kelvin produces heat. But it is by no means \\"dead.\\" It has simply moved into different realms. The book even quotes some modern philosophers, and not altogether disparagingly, later on. Regardless, few feathers will ruffle by pronouncing that the love of wisdom has expired. But the book's other \\"bold\\" claim made headlines. According to the authors, current theory has rendered a creator, or \\"God,\\" as redundant and extraneous. We no longer need the old Deus ex Machina to explain the origins of all that exists. This will shock no one who has followed modern science. But rarely has this message been splattered so copiously across headlines. Some in the Christian community accused Hawking and co-author Milodinov of trying to increase sales through religious sensationalism. Maybe the publishers had these sections juiced up to help sales in today's stagnant book market? Maybe. Who knows? Truth is, this book would sell with or without any reference to God. But doubtless the controversy (or pseudo-controversy) caused a few more to fly from shelves. But focusing on these minor points detracts from the meat of the book: M-theory.<br /><br />We came from somewhere. An obvious enough statement, but the answer still eludes us. To answer the question \\"why there is something rather than nothing\\" the book first provides science history lesson. By starting with questions around the rule of law and debates concerning the nature of reality, the first few chapters culminate in a worldview called \\"Model-dependent realism.\\" According to this view, we can only know the world through interpretive frameworks. No single objectivity exists. Some may cry \\"relativism\\" here, but models or frameworks must correspond to observation. Nonetheless, multiple frameworks may agree with observation. How do we clear this up? To use the book's example, Copernicus did not prove Ptolemy wrong, he just provided a simpler model of reality. \\"Classical\\" physics posited one, and only one, reality. This new view embraces anti-realism and allows multiple realities. And not only multiple realities, but multiple histories. Quantum physics, particularly the thoughts of Richard Feynman, argues that the subatomic world acts in counter-intuitive ways. The double-slit experiment's famous interference patterns introduced these startling revelations. Add the implications of Relativity theory to this and the universe takes on surprising new qualities, including the possibility of multiple dimensions. Here the book introduces M-theory (what \\"M\\" stands for remains unclear), allegedly the only candidate for a new \\"theory of everything,\\" though it actually comprises many theories. The M-theory universe has 11 dimensions, many of them \\"rolled up\\" beyond everyday detection, and multiple universes with different laws. And \\"multiple\\" here means billions and trillions. Quantum fluctuations produce a myriad of universes out ofnothing. Yes, M-theory says that we ultimately come from nothing. Well, the universes do, humans derive from the nuclear ovens inside of stars, where fusion of hydrogen and helium eventually produces carbon, which gets blasted out when the star erupts as a Supernova. But the universe has to form \\"just right\\" for life to exist. The final few chapters show our precarious position. Astronomer Martin Rees calls us the \\"nuclear waste\\" of the stars. If some elements of our universe were adjusted even slightly, life as we know it would never have arisen. So obviously the universe was designed for us, right? Not according to M-theory. Countless universes bubble out of the void. Some collapse, some expand. Some are conducive to life, others not. We just happen to inhabit one of the universes that supports life. Billions of others don't. And in a nearly infinite sea of universes, the chance that our \\"special\\" universe would develop becomes more than a remote possibility, in fact it becomes almost inevitable. This reduces the \\"miracle\\" of our existence down to quantum fluctuations, again out of our primordial origin, nothing. M-theory has not yet accumulated enough observational confirmation to dominate cosmology. If it does then \\"we will have found the grand design.\\" Time and experiment will tell.<br /><br />\\"The Grand Design\\" makes the inaccessible accessible. Though the book gets technical in places, especially in chapter 5, general readers will have little difficulty completing it. Some people may get too wound up in the theological implications of M-theory, but that issue remains marginal throughout. This is not a \\"science vs. religion\\" book, though some of the extended \\"God\\" passages may create that false impression. Though provocative and effective for stirring controversy, the book didn't really need to mention the \\"creator\\" implications of M-theory. Such implications are implicit in the theory itself and arguably irrelevant. Nonetheless, those looking for a great introduction to contemporary scientific cosmology will find a great example in \\"The Grand Design.\\" Short, readable, entertaining, even funny in places, the book will hopefully garner a large readership of the scienfically curious but uninitiated. Those with a physics or cosmology background will likely learn nothing new here. But general readers will ingest droves of fascinating information, regardless of M-theory's ultimate standing or feasability. And who knows, maybe we did come from nothing? That certainly would explain a lot.Yes, M-theory says that we ultimately come from nothing. 0385732554|4.0|First, I want to thank Gretchen Rubin, whose The Happiness Project led me to The Giver. It was on a list of children's books she loved. I think it should be on any thoughtful adult's list as well. Jonas is chosen to hold all the memories in a world where everything is ordered and organized in such a way that no one needs to make decisions. Hunger, want, violence, chaos do not exist in this well-ordered society and its members are spared as well the pain of imperfect children and frail elders. No one questions anything - except Jonas. And as he is given all the memories that exist, he increasingly questions how society is organized and whether or not people would be better remembering for themselves.<br />This is a though-provoking book for anyone who has wrestled with the thorny issues around societal control and information flow. Who owns our memories? How much information should be shared? It may be that this is essential reading for anyone who is trying to figure out social media and anyone who has ever reflected on the appropriate boundaries for government. It certainly broadened my perspective.

But then the movie feels the need to have an antagonist.<br /><br />Charles Muntz is a poorly handled villain in a movie that didn't need one. He's spent years in South America looking for this cryptid bird, and he's become so afraid that someone will steal his glory that he's ready to kill. It's ridiculous think he wouldn't want to join up with other explorers and make his life easier. He may want all the credit, but to turn evil? He's rather vengeful toward to the bird, which seems to be outside the nature of this kind of character. He cares about his dogs, but not a bird that has pretty much dominated his life for years? How could he NOT have caught this thing by now? If he's never owned chocolate, how come Beta and Gamma could recognize its smell? Why doesn't he show the world his other amazing discoveries and become famous again? He could have been portrayed as a willing but broken old man who just wants to do something meaningful. 0786880708|5.0|As a rule, I do not like self-help books. Kabat makes few such promises, and I have found that his methods deliver more results than anything you can buy anywhere else on the market.  He tells you how meditation and mindfullness works, not where it will get you in life.  Instead of selling large and radical adjustments to your life, he advocates for small changes, most of which are a shift in one's perception of how to approach one's internal life.  He gives methods and ideas that enable one to step aside from one's self and one's daily regiment in life while not becoming detached from the emotions entalied therein (a far cry from other self-help authors).  Resistance to the dynamics within oneself are discouraged; rather, one is encouraged to embrace all that one feels throughout the day and while in meditation.  I have struggled personally for all of my life, since childhood, and I can honestly say that this book has worked for me.  In fact, I have found the author to be so helpful that I need only read at most 20 pages about every one or two weeks.  I started this book at the beginning of 2010. It is now October, and I still have maybe 100 pages to go.  All the while I have managed to stay afloat some terrible moments and to struggle through them to find hope.  This book deserves some credit for helping me get through these times.  That's what I call getting your money's worth.

0525951652|5.0|The jacket forthrightly tells us that \\"Fall of Giants\\" is Book 1 of the Century Trilogy.  It is of a similar length to \\"The Pillars of the Earth\\" and \\"World Without End\\", Follett's two previous exercises in blockbuster historical fiction; assuming the length holds in the remaining installments, the saga will be around 3000 pages when it concludes in 2014.  \\"Fall of Giants\\" is not Follett's best work (that would be \\"Pillars\\", in my estimation), but assuming that he can keep up the same level of quality, the Century Trilogy may yet end up as his magnum opus, if for no other reason than sheer scope and length.  Spoilers follow.    The basic conceit is to follow five families through the entire 20th century (well, it starts in 1911, but close enough):  there are the Williams siblings, Ethel and Billy, the children of Welsh coal miners; The Earl Fitzherbert and Lady Maud, Anglo-Welsh aristocratic siblings; Grigori and Lev Peshkov, Russian peasant brothers; Walter von Ulrich, a German aristocrat; and Gus Dewar, the son of a US senator.  And each of these five groups come with parents, friends, and other attendant supporting characters; no wonder the novel has a multi-page index of everybody involved, akin to one of Tolstoy's long works.  The novel opens with the coronation of King George V, contrasted with Billy's first descent into the coal mine, and follows the characters through the First World War, the Russian Revolution, and all the social tumult that accompanied it.  One may detect echoes of some of Follett's earlier novels here; in particular, the interaction between the Fitzherberts and the Williamses will undoubtedly remind readers of \\"The Man from St. Petersburg\\", Follett's only other novel set in this timeframe.    The usual Follett themes are here, from women's rights to the rise of the Labour Party (I know Mrs. Follett is a Labour MP; he sometimes comes close to writing Labour Party propaganda here).  Follett's earlier historical novels' most prominent themeis showing how for most of history rich douchebags have been able to do pretty much whatever they wanted so long as they didn't anger other rich douchebags; here we're getting into the era of full democracy, and things are starting to change (the final scene is pretty pointed on this).  Russia obviously experiences the severest case of this, and Follett commendably attempts to delineate the complexity of the revolution and peoples' reactions to it around the world (how a completely legitimate overthrow of a brutal dictatorship ultimately ended up creating another).  Britain makes a more peaceable, but still difficult, transition to full democracy, breaking down the class barriers that had previously existed (Follett, for all his Labour sympathies, does manage to make his main Conservative a reasonably well-drawn character).    By the novel's end, you can see Follett marshalling all the families into position to play major roles in the next great cataclysm, the Second World War, albeit on occasion with perhaps a little too much irony (actual dialogue, on one character being informed that Adolf Hitler has been arrested in the aftermath of the failed 1924 Munich Beer Hall putsch:  \\"Well, thank God that's over.\\").  I will say that, given Follett's stated ambition of tracking his characters through the big sweep of history, there's not a huge amount of time spent in America in this story (Gus Dewar spends most of his time in Europe; one other character does get there, and is set up to be in the thick of Prohibition by the story's end), particularly in contrast to the vivid and thorough pictures painted of socio-political change in the UK and Russia.    Recommended.

B00026WU3M|5.0|Bob Dylan's 1989 release \\"Oh Mercy\\" was an important release in several ways. It marked his first collaboration with Daniel Lanois, who went on to produce the album that launched Dylan's meteoric comeback, 1997's \\"Time Out of Mind.\\" It was also the album that pulled him out of the personal and artistic rut he had been in following 1983's \\"Infidels.\\"<br /><br />After the abysmal quadrilogy of \\"Empire Burlesque,\\" \\"Knocked Out Loaded,\\" \\"Down in the Groove,\\" and \\"Dylan and the Dead,\\" \\"Oh Mercy\\" felt like a breath of fresh air for longtime Dylan fans. Not since \\"Desire\\" had he come up with a work this consistent. Starting with \\"Street Legal,\\" Dylan's album had started to become a hodge podge of top quality tracks mixed with dire filer. For every \\"Changing of the Guards,\\" there was \\"New Pony.\\" For every \\"Dark Eyes,\\" there was clean cut kid. For every \\"Brownsville Girl,\\" there was the rest of \\"Knocked Out Loaded.\\" \\"Oh Mercy\\" only has one real clunker (\\"Everything is Broken\\" - how this made the cut over \\"Dignity\\" is a mystery to me), while everything else is top shelf Dylan of the highest order, from the venomous opening \\"Political World\\" to the soul piercing closer \\"Shooting Star.\\"<br /><br />Lanois' swampy production meshes well with the mostly dreary compositions, such as the chilling \\"Man in the Long Black Coat\\" and the soul bearing \\"What Good Am I?\\" and \\"Disease of Conceit,\\" both revealing insights into Dylan's crumbling personal life at the time. \\"Ring Them Bells\\" is one of Dylan's finest compositions, a desparate prayer that is far more lyrically rich and emotionally sincere than anything from Dylan's short lived and inconsistent Born Again phase. \\"Where Teardrops Fall\\" and \\"What Was It You Wanted\\" are both solid, above average.<br /><br />\\"Oh Mercy\\" signaled a positive change in Dylan's artistic direction, one that would once again establish his importance and relevance as the most important voice in rock.

B000002LRX|5.0|Angel Dust, from Faith No More, is unquestionably in the top 5 best rock albums ever created. Ironically, it was also written and recorded when the band was at its most unstable. Guitarist Jim Martin was on the verge of quitting, and producer Matt Wallace has commented most of the instruments were tracked in the studio at different times as opposed to the entire band recording together. Regardless, from start to finish, each song is a meticulously crafted masterpiece, no two sounding similar unlike the band's previous effort, \\"The Real Thing.\\" I got this album a year or two after it came out, when I was in Jr. High School. I remember thinking on first listen that \\"Land  of Sunshine\\" was possibly the best rock song I'd ever heard. The rest of the album, save for \\"Midlife Crisis\\" took a few listens to grow on me but it wasn't long before \\"Angel Dust\\" shot to the top of my \\"best rock albums\\" list. Early favorites were the two aforementioned tracks in addition to the strange yet driving \\"Crack Hitler,\\" \\"A Small Victory,\\" the appropriately energetic \\"Caffeine,\\" and the beautiful disaster \\"Malpractice.\\" 15 years later, it hasn't slipped from the #1 spot despite my musical horizons expanding tenfold.    Faith No More's biggest strength in my eyes is their ability to reinvent themselves on every single album. That said, they never released such a perfect collection of songs as they did with \\"Angel Dust.\\" AD is a dark album, with many of the lyrics addressing vocalist Mike Patton's strained relationship with his father, as well songs written during periods of drug experimentation. In that regard, I suppose the album was aptly titled. Faith No More's other \\"dark\\" album was their 1985 debut \\"We Care a Lot,\\" and musically foreshadowed some of the themes that would be fully realized on \\"Angel Dust.\\"    One of the highlights of \\"Angel Dust\\" is the perfect vocal performance by Patton. He uses his voice like an instrument, and has a unique talent unmatched by any other vocalist inthe realm of rock. While the album failed to match the sales of its predecessor \\"The Real Thing,\\" it did net the band their only #1 hit, \\"Midlife Crisis.\\"     Oddly enough, \\"Angel Dust\\" was huge all over the world.... except in the USA. I like to think this is because the musical direction of the album was too cerebral for the single-oriented American masses. Because of this, the band gained huge followings in Europe and South America that continue to this day. The American version of \\"Angel Dust\\" only features 13 tracks whereas import versions contain a full 15 song tracklist. The two tracks omitted on the US version are, in my opinion, a must-- a cover of the Commodore's \\"Easy\\" (possibly the band's most well-known song after \\"Epic\\"), and a re-recorded version of \\"As the Worm Turns\\" from \\"We Care a Lot.\\" The new version contains a much superior vocal performance in line with the rest of the \\"Angel Dust\\" album, but lacks the piano intro on the older version. \\"Angel Dust\\" is an album you have to listen to from start to finish, and these two tracks are an essential part of the experience.    \\"Angel Dust\\" isn't just a musical masterpiece; it was a ground breaking record in the world of rock. The album effectively paved the way for the entire \\"NuMetal\\" genre which would become extremely popular in the late '90s and early 2000s, although none of these bands could match the talent and originality of Faith No More. \\"Angel Dust\\" rocks hard from start to finish, climaxing (appropriately) in the guitar-laden \\"Jizzlobber.\\" Occasionally veering off the path for piano numbers like \\"RV,\\" or easy listening ballads like \\"Easy,\\" the album keeps you wondering what sort of musical genius will surface on the next track. The album is also keyboard and synth heavy, definitely the best performance from resident FNM keyboardist Roddy Bottum. I can't think of any other band where the keyboard plays as big a role in the music as Faith No More. Drug problems and inter-band relations meant there wasn't much input from Roddy on \\"Angel Dust's\\" followup, \\"King for A Day, Fool For a Lifetime\\" and the resulting absence of keyboards is glaringly obvious.    Newcomers to Faith No More ought to start with \\"Angel Dust,\\" as it is more indicative of the direction the band took during their glory years. While \\"The Real Thing\\" was a bigger commercial success, it sounds extremely dated and...... well, commercial. Overproduced and slick, \\"The Real Thing\\" doesn't sound like anything else in their catalog. In that vein, people who wrote the band off as a cheesy 80s act after hearing TRT need to give Faith No More another chance and pick up \\"Angel Dust.\\" Don't just take my word for it..... Take the word of the five star AVERAGE this album has here on Amazon. Just be sure to find an import version that contains all 15 tracks.

1594488843|2.0|'Drive' offers little in the way of new information about motivation in the workplace. Much of the research that Pink cites (e.g., Deci & Ryan), when he cites any at all, has gross flaws in experimental design, lacks external validity (not conducted in the workplace) in this case, and ignores basic behavioral principles that even a new student of behavioral psychology would be familiar.  Most of the basic concepts presented in this book are consistent with the human relations school of thought, which is only really half alive today.  If you want to understand the science of rewards (both extrinsic and intrinsic) and are seeking a balanced perspective on both financial and non-financial rewards (recognition, praise) read Aubrey Daniel's book entitled Performance Management (warning: it's a bit technical, but not dry).  Financial rewards systems typically fail because they are poorly designed and implemented.  In addition to mastering the use of non-financial rewards to motivate employees (and simply to be human), you may also want to read up on the history of \\"compensation\\" and more effective pay alternatives than just paying employees for an 8-hour work day, regardless of how little they produce or how well they produce it (e.g., paying employees for productivity, quality, creativity, efficiency etc).  I'd recommend Bill Abernathy's 'The Sin of Wages'.  Drive is generic!  If we're going to solve workplace problems then we need something more innovative than the same ol' rehashed stuff!
